Or from dirhams, so he did, and she had nothing in her hand, then upon her is three dirhams. [2015 (11)] And if she said: 'Divorce me three times for one thousand' and he divorced her once, then upon her is one-third of the thousand. If the plural form is used, then at minimum three numbers are binding. This ruling is based on the principle that when the particle 'ba' is used for exchange, the compensation is divided over the object of exchange. There is an athar from al-Shu'ba [al-Thawri] about a man whose wife said 'Sell me three divorces for one thousand dirhams' and he divorced her once and then refused, so he gets one-third of the thousand, and she is irrevocably divorced once. Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling on khul' when the wife requests multiple divorces for a lump sum but receives fewer. The ruling: if she says 'Divorce me three times for one thousand' and he divorces her once, she owes one-third of the thousand. The principle is that the particle 'ba' (ب) indicates exchange, and the compensation is divided proportionally over the number of divorces. This is supported by an athar from al-Thawri and al-Zuhri. The divorce becomes irrevocable (ba'in) because it is given in exchange for compensation.

Key Terms
ب (ba): The particle used to indicate the exchange (badaliyyah) in khul' transactions.
بدلیت (badaliyyah): Exchange; the concept that the compensation is in exchange for the divorces.

Dispute on Compensation When Wife Uses 'Ala (Condition) for Three Divorces
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation in khul' with conditional wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on compensation when a wife uses 'ala for three divorces, and explain the legal consequence of each view.

Core Idea
The particle 'ala indicates a condition; if the condition is not fulfilled, no compensation is due.

What happens when a wife asks for three divorces in exchange for money but only receives one? The answer depends on a single Arabic particle. In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute where the interpretation of 'ala (على) determines whether the wife owes compensation and whether the divorce is revocable or irrevocable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Issue
The issue is whether compensation is due when a wife uses the particle 'ala to request three divorces for a sum, but only one divorce is given.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and His Dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that no compensation is due because 'ala indicates a condition (shart). The condition of three divorces was not fulfilled, so the promise to pay is void. He cites the athar of al-Thawri as evidence.
The athar explicitly states that if less than three divorces are given, nothing is due.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Their Dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that one-third of the sum is due because they interpret 'ala as equivalent to 'ba (exchange). The sum is divided proportionally over the divorces.
They view the wording as a contract of khul' where each divorce carries a portion of the compensation.
4
Analyze the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Difference)
According to Abu Hanifa, the single divorce is raj'i (revocable) with no compensation. According to Sahibayn, it is ba'in (irrevocable) and the wife owes one-third of the sum.
This affects the ability to remarry and the financial obligation.
5
Note Any Tarjih
The text does not specify a tarjih. In later Hanafi fatwa, Abu Hanifa's view is often preferred when the condition is explicit, but both positions are valid in the madhhab.
The mufti should weigh the wording and context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۰۱۶ (۱۲) اور اگر کہا مجھے تین طلاقیں دیں ہزار کی شرط پر ، پس طلاق دی اس کو ایک تو عورت پر کچھ لازم نہیں ہوگا امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ علی شرط کے لئے آتا ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ تین طلاق کی شرط پر ایک ہزار دینے کا وعدہ کیا اور شرط پوری نہیں ہوئی۔ کیونکہ شوہر نے ایک ہی طلاق دی اس لئے شوہر کو کچھ نہیں ملے گا۔ کیونکہ شرط نہیں پائی گئی۔ اور یہاں ہزار تین طلاقوں پر تقسیم نہیں ہو گا (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن الشورى وان قالت له اعطيك الف درهم على ان تطلقنى ثلاثا ، فان طلق ثلاثا كان له الف درهم ، وان طلق واحدة او اثنتين لم يكن له شيء وهو احق بها (ب) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الفداء بالشر ط ج سادس ص ۴۹۴ نمبر ۱۱۸۰۶) اس اثر میں ہے کہ علی استعمال کیا اور تین طلاق کی شرط پر ایک ہزار دینے کا وعدہ کیا اور شوہر نے ایک طلاق دی تو عورت پر کچھ بھی لازم نہیں ہوگا ، اور طلاق رجعی واقع ہوگی، کیونکہ کسی بدلے کے بغیر طلاق واقع ہوئی۔
اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ شرط مشروط پر تقسیم نہیں ہوگی۔
حاشیہ : (ب) حضرت ثوری نے فرمایا اگر عورت نے شوہر سے کہا میں آپ کو ایک ہزار دیتی ہوں اس شرط پر کہ مجھے تین طلاقیں دیں، پس اگر طلاق دی تین تو اس کے لئے ایک ہزار ہے۔ اور اگر طلاق دی ایک یادو تو شوہر کے لئے کچھ نہیں ہوگا۔ اور شوہر عورت کا زیادہ حقدار ہے یعنی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔

[۲۰۱۷ (۱۳) وقالا رحمهما الله تعالى عليها ثُلث الالف اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ عورت پر ہزار کی تہائی لازم ہوگی ۔ وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہاں بھی علی ، ب کے معنی میں ہے، اور بدلیت کے معنی میں ہے۔ اس لئے اس صورت میں بھی ہر ایک طلاق پر ہزار قسیم ہو جائے گا۔ اور ایک طلاق پر ایک تہائی رقم لازم ہوگی ۔
[2016 (12)] And if she said: 'Divorce me three times on condition of one thousand' and he divorced her once, then nothing is due upon her according to Abu Hanifa. 'Ala is for condition; its conclusion is that she promised to give one thousand on the condition of three divorces, and the condition was not fulfilled because the husband gave only one divorce, so he gets nothing. And here the thousand is not divided over the divorces. There is an athar: al-Shu'ba [al-Thawri] reported that a woman said to her husband: 'I give you one thousand dirhams on the condition that you divorce me three times.' If he divorces three times, he gets the thousand; if he divorces once or twice, he gets nothing and he has more right to her (i.e., no divorce occurs). This athar shows that when 'ala is used, if the condition is not met, nothing is due. The principle: a condition is not divided over the conditioned. Footnote: al-Thawri said: if a woman says 'I give you one thousand on condition you divorce me three times' and he divorces once or twice, he gets nothing and the divorce does not occur. [2017 (13)] And the two (Sahibayn) said: upon her is one-third of the thousand. They say that here too 'ala has the meaning of 'ba' (exchange), so the thousand is divided over each divorce, and for one divorce, one-third is due.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the meaning of the particle 'ala in khul'. Abu Hanifa considers 'ala as indicating a condition (shart), so if the condition of three divorces is not fulfilled, no compensation is due. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) interpret 'ala as equivalent to 'ba' indicating exchange, so the compensation is proportional. The athar from al-Thawri supports Abu Hanifa's view. The practical consequence: according to Abu Hanifa, if only one divorce is given, it is revocable (raj'i) because no compensation was paid; according to Sahibayn, it is irrevocable (ba'in) and she owes one-third. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is not specified here, but later Hanafi works prefer Abu Hanifa's view in matters of condition.

Key Terms
على ('ala): In this context, it indicates a condition (shart) according to Abu Hanifa, or exchange (badal) according to Sahibayn.
شرط (shart): Condition; the payment is conditional on the occurrence of three divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a wife says 'Divorce me three times on condition of one thousand dirhams' and the husband divorces her once, what is the ruling?
A) She owes one-third of the thousand and the divorce is ba'in.
B) She owes nothing and the divorce is raj'i.
C) She owes the full thousand.
D) The divorce is invalid.

Key Takeaway
When a wife uses the particle 'ala for a conditional khul', the condition must be fully fulfilled for compensation to be due according to Abu Hanifa.

Partial Fulfillment of Delegated Divorce (Tafwid) is Ineffective
Ruling on partial compliance in delegated divorce (tafwid).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband delegates talaq to his wife with a condition (e.g., for a sum) and she divorces herself fewer times than specified, and explain why no divorce occurs.

Core Idea
A conditional delegation of talaq is invalid if the wife does not fulfill the exact condition.

In Hanafi fiqh, a husband may delegate the power to divorce to his wife (tafwid). However, if he sets a specific condition such as divorcing three times for a sum, the wife must comply exactly. This lesson examines the case where she divorces once instead of three, and the ruling that neither divorce nor payment occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its terms
The husband tells his wife 'Divorce yourself three times for one thousand' or 'on condition of one thousand'. This is a delegation (tafwid) with a binding condition.
The Arabic words 'bi' (for) and 'ala' (on) both indicate condition here.
2
Understand the condition
The husband's intention is to get three divorces in exchange for the full sum. He would not agree to less. The condition is integral to the delegation.
3
Analyze the partial performance
The wife divorces herself only once. This does not satisfy the condition of three divorces. The act is incomplete relative to what was required.
The condition is considered 'against her'—it burdens her to perform fully.
4
Conclude the ruling
Since the condition is not met, no divorce occurs (the delegation is void), and the wife owes nothing because the condition was not fulfilled.
This applies whether the husband said 'for' or 'on condition'.

Source Text

شوہر نے بیوی سے کہا کہ تم اپنے آپ کو ایک ہزار کے بدلے تین طلاق دے دو۔ عورت نے ایک طلاق دی تو عورت پر کوئی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ اور شوہر کو تہائی رقم بھی نہیں ملے گی۔ چاہے ہزار کے بدلے میں کہے یا ہزار کی شرط پر کہے دونوں صورتوں میں یہاں شرط کے معنی میں ہے۔ کیونکہ شوہر ہزار سے کم پر راضی نہیں ہوگا۔ اور ایک تہائی رقم پر عورت کو جدا کرنے پر راضی نہیں ہوگا۔ اس لئے عورت کے خلاف شرط کرنے پر نہ طلاق واقع ہوگی اور نہ عورت پر کچھ لازم ہوگا۔
[2018 (14)] And if the husband said: 'Divorce yourself three times for one thousand or on one thousand' and she divorced herself once, nothing of divorce occurs upon her. The husband told his wife to divorce herself three times for one thousand dirhams. She gave one divorce, so no divorce occurs, and the husband gets no one-third. Whether he said 'for' or 'on condition', here the meaning is condition, because the husband would not be satisfied with less than a thousand, nor would he be satisfied to separate her for one-third. Therefore, since the condition is against her, neither divorce occurs nor is anything due upon her.

Commentary
This ruling concerns the case where the husband delegates the power of talaq to the wife, telling her to divorce herself three times for a specific sum or on a condition. If she divorces herself only once, the ruling is that no divorce falls and she owes nothing. The reason is that the husband's intention is clearly to get the full sum for three divorces; he would not agree to less. Therefore, the partial act does not fulfill the condition, so it is as if nothing happened. This applies whether he uses 'ba' (for) or 'ala' (on condition), because in this context both are interpreted as a condition. The divorce does not occur, and no compensation is due.

Key Terms
تفویض (tafwid): Delegation of the power to divorce to the wife by the husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One divorce occurs and she owes one-third.
B) No divorce occurs and she owes nothing.
C) Three divorces occur because she acted.
D) One divorce occurs but she owes nothing.

Key Takeaway
A conditional delegation of talaq requires exact fulfillment; partial compliance renders the act void.

Khul' and Mubara'ah Drop All Spousal Rights
General ruling with two athar proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that khul' and mubara'ah cancel all marital financial rights, and identify the athar evidence supporting it.

Core Idea
Khul' and mubara'ah erase all marital financial rights—including iddah maintenance and mahr.

This lesson addresses a key principle in Hanafi family law: when a marriage ends through khul' (wife-initiated divorce with compensation) or mubara'ah (mutual release), all financial rights arising from the marriage are cancelled. This includes the wife's right to iddah maintenance, housing, and unpaid mahr. The reasoning is that in khul', the husband already receives compensation from the wife, and in mubara'ah, both parties mutually release each other. We will examine the ruling and the athar evidence cited by the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage says about khul' and mubara'ah. It states that both forms of separation drop every right that each spouse has against the other.
Focus on the legal effect: saqit (dropped).
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites athars (statements of the Salaf) from Qatada and al-Sha'bi. Find the exact Arabic texts and their sources.
These athars are considered mursal hadith, but accepted as evidence in the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the athars prove the ruling. Qatada explicitly negates maintenance (nafaqah) for the woman of khul' and mubara'ah. Al-Sha'bi argues that since the husband takes from her, he cannot be required to spend on her.
The logical contradiction is the key proof.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Hanafi position is that all rights are dropped. However, other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may hold that iddah maintenance is still due if the wife is pregnant. Identify the point of disagreement.
Cross-madhhab difference affects practical application.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the apparent meaning of the athars and the analogy (qiyas) that khul' and mubara'ah are like a sale where mutual release ends obligations. This is the mufta bihi position.
The ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah and is considered strong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[2019 (15)] And mubara'ah is like khul', and khul' and mubara'ah drop every right that each has against the other. These rights that arise from the marriage are all dropped due to khul' and mutual discharge. For example, iddah maintenance, shelter, mahr etc. are not obligatory upon the husband. Mubara'ah means that the wife is free from all the husband's rights and the husband is free from all the wife's rights, so both become free from all rights. And in khul', the husband takes from the wife, so how could he give her? There is evidence in athar: Qatada said: 'The divorced woman by khul' and the woman by mubara'ah have no maintenance.' Commentary
This passage establishes a key principle of Hanafi family law: when a marriage ends through khul' (divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation) or mubara'ah (mutual release), all financial rights arising from the marriage are cancelled. This includes the wife's right to iddah maintenance (nafaqah), housing (sakan), and unpaid mahr. The reasoning is that in khul', the husband is already receiving compensation from the wife, so it would be contradictory to require him to also provide maintenance. The same applies to mubara'ah where both parties mutually release each other. The ruling is supported by athars from Qatada and al-Sha'bi.

Key Terms
مبارأة (mubara'ah): Mutual release between spouses from all marriage-related rights and obligations.
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.

Key Takeaway
Khul' and mubara'ah terminate all financial rights from marriage, including iddah maintenance, housing, and mahr, as per the Hanafi school.

Conditional Khul' and Partial Divorce: The Sahibayn's Two Rulings
Rulings on partial performance in khul' by the Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on partial performance in a conditional khul' and explain how the interpretation of the preposition 'ala determines the outcome.

Core Idea
In khul', whether a partial divorce triggers partial payment depends on whether the husband’s words are interpreted as a condition (shart) or an exchange (badal).

The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) address a subtle point in khul' transactions: what happens when the husband demands three divorces for a fixed compensation, but the wife only divorces herself once? The answer hinges on the meaning of the Arabic preposition 'ala. This lesson unpacks their rulings and the underlying linguistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The husband says 'Divorce yourself three times for (or upon) a thousand.' In the first scenario, the wife gives one divorce and the Sahibayn rule she owes one-third. In the second, she gives one divorce and they rule no divorce and no payment. The difference is the interpretation of 'ala.
2
Analyze the husband's intent
When 'ala means 'in exchange for' (ba), each divorce is a separate transaction with a proportionate price. When it means 'on condition of' (shart), the full three divorces are required for any payment.
3
Apply to scenario (13)
Here 'ala is interpreted as ba, so the thousand is divided equally among the three divorces. The wife owes one-third for one divorce and that divorce falls.
4
Apply to scenario (14)
Here 'ala is interpreted as shart; the husband is unwilling to accept less than the full thousand for three divorces. Since she only gave one, the condition is not met, so no divorce occurs and no payment is due.
5
Distinguish the linguistic principle
The context and common usage determine the meaning. The Sahibayn use linguistic analysis to infer the husband's intent, a key method in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۳) وقالا رحمهما الله تعالى عليها ثُلث الالف ( اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ عورت پر ہزار کی تہائی لازم ہوگی ۔ وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ یہاں بھی علی ، ب کے معنی میں ہے، اور بدلیت کے معنی میں ہے۔ اس لئے اس صورت میں بھی ہر ایک طلاق پر ہزار قسیم ہو جائے گا۔ اور ایک طلاق پر ایک تہائی رقم لازم ہوگی ۔ ) (۱۴) ولو قال الزوج طلقی نفسک ثلثا بالف او على الف فطلقت نفسها واحدة لم يقع عليها شيء من الطلاق ( شوہر نے بیوی سے کہا کہ تم اپنے آپ کو ایک ہزار کے بدلے تین طلاق دے دو۔ عورت نے ایک طلاق دی تو عورت پر کوئی طلاق واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ اور شوہر کو تہائی رقم بھی نہیں ملے گی۔ چاہے ہزار کے بدلے میں کہے یا ہزار کی شرط پر کہے دونوں صورتوں میں یہاں شرط کے معنی میں ہے۔ کیونکہ شوہر ہزار سے کم پر راضی نہیں ہوگا۔ اور ایک تہائی رقم پر عورت کو جدا کرنے پر راضی نہیں ہوگا۔ اس لئے عورت کے خلاف شرط کرنے پر نہ طلاق واقع ہوگی اور نہ عورت پر کچھ لازم ہوگا۔ )
(13) And both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said, may Allah have mercy on them, that upon her is one-third of the thousand. (14) And if the husband said: 'Divorce yourself three times for a thousand' or 'upon a thousand' and she divorced herself once, nothing of divorce falls upon her.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings from the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding conditional khul'. In the first ruling, the husband says 'divorce yourself three times for a thousand' (using the word 'ala) and she gives only one divorce. According to the Sahibayn, the word 'ala here has the meaning of the preposition 'ba' (exchange or price), so each divorce is assigned a part of the thousand. Consequently, she owes one-third of the thousand (333.33). This is based on the principle that when a price is set for a bundle of items, each item takes a proportionate share of the total price. In the second ruling, the husband uses the same expression but the wife divorces once. Here, the word 'ala is interpreted as 'shart' (condition) meaning that the husband is not willing to accept less than the full thousand for the three divorces. Since he did not consent to a partial divorce for a partial payment, neither divorce falls nor is any money owed. The reasoning is that the husband's statement is a conditional offer: if you give three divorces, you pay the full amount; if you give less, the condition is not met. Thus, the wife's action does not fulfill the condition, so the khul' fails entirely. The difference between the two cases hinges on the context and the intention behind the husband's words, as interpreted by the Sahibayn.

Key Terms
الخلع (al-khul'): Divorce at the wife's request in exchange for compensation paid by the wife to the husband.
على ('ala): A preposition interpreted in this context as either exchange (ب) or condition (شرط) by the Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One divorce falls and she owes one-third of the thousand.
B) No divorce falls and she owes nothing.
C) One divorce falls and she owes the full thousand.
D) Three divorces fall and she owes the full thousand.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'ala as exchange or condition is decisive in partial performance of khul', leading to either proportionate payment or complete failure of the transaction.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Rights Nullified by Khul' and Mubara'ah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on rights nullification by khul' and mubara'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions on which marital rights are nullified by khul' and mubara'ah and apply the mufta bihi view in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Khul' and mubara'ah nullify only specified rights — the school's preferred view protects the wife's default entitlements.

In this passage, we encounter an intra-Hanafi disagreement. While all three founding imams agree that khul' and mubara'ah are valid forms of separation, they differ sharply on what happens to the wife's financial rights. The dispute turns on the interpretation of prophetic practices (athar) and the linguistic scope of 'nullification'. Understanding this dispute is essential for anyone who will later issue fatwas or draft separation agreements, because the operative ruling (mufta bihi) follows Imam Muhammad's position, not Abu Hanifa's.

Guided Path
1
State the common ground
Identify what is not disputed: khul' and mubara'ah are valid forms of separation requiring compensation from the wife. The disagreement is only on the extent to which they nullify spousal rights arising from the marriage (nafaqah, sakan, mahr, etc.).
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position and its proof
Abu Hanifa holds that both khul' and mubara'ah automatically nullify all rights between spouses. His proof is an athar from Qatada: 'The mukhala'ah and mubara'ah have no nafaqah.' Also reasoning: if the husband takes compensation, he cannot be required to spend.
This is the most extreme view; it strips the wife of all iddah financial rights.
3
Identify Abu Yusuf's position and its reasoning
Abu Yusuf distinguishes: mubara'ah (by its linguistic meaning of mutual freeing) nullifies all rights automatically. Khul' does not carry that implication and thus only nullifies rights that are explicitly specified in the agreement.
For khul', rights not mentioned remain intact.
4
Identify Muhammad's position and its proof
Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that neither khul' nor mubara'ah nullify any rights automatically; only those rights that the parties explicitly specify in the agreement are waived. His proof is an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i: 'The mukhala'ah is entitled to sakan and nafaqah.' This indicates default preservation unless waived.
This is the most protective of the wife's rights.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, wife loses all; under Abu Yusuf, only mubara'ah strips all; under Muhammad, nothing is stripped without specification. The later Hanafi school adopted Muhammad's view as mufta bihi for its precautionary approach favoring the wife.
In fatwa, a wife retains all iddah rights unless explicitly waived in the khul' or mubara'ah agreement.

نفقة ام لا؟ ج رابع ص ۱۲۷، نمبر ۱۸۴۹۳ر مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب نفقة المتلعة الحامل ج سادس ص ۵۰۷ نمبر ۱۱۸۶۳) اس اثر میں ہے کہ ضلع اور مبارات میں شوہر سے نفقہ ساقط ہو جائے گا (۲) عــن الـشـعـبـي سـئـل عن المختلعة لها نفقة ؟ فقال كيف ينفق عليها وهو يأخذ منها (ب) (مصنف ابن ابى شبيبة ۱۱۳ حاشیہ : (الف) ضلع والی کے لئے اور مبارات کرنے والی کے لئے نفقہ نہیں ہے (ب) حضرت معصی سے پوچھا کیا ضلع کرنے والی کو نفقہ ملے گا؟ فرمایا اس پر کیسے خرچ کرے گا اس سے تو لے رہا ہے۔ ) (16) وقال ابو یوسف رحمه الله تعالى المبارأة تسقط ( اور امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا مبارات ساقط کرتا ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں مبارات سے حقوق زوجین ساقط ہوں گے خلع سے ساقط نہیں ہوں گے۔ مبارات کے معنی ہی ہیں ایک دوسرے کو ہر حقوق سے بری کرنا۔ اس لئے اس سے ساقط ہو جائیں گے ۔ اور ضلع میں متعین کرے کہ فلاں فلاں حقوق ساقط ہوں گے تو وہ ساقط ہو جائیں گے۔ اور جو متعین نہ کرے وہ ساقط نہیں ہوں گے۔ کیونکہ ضلع میں تمام حقوق کو ساقط کرنے کے معنی نہیں ہیں ۔ ) (17) وقال محمد رحمه الله تعالى لا تسقطان الا ما سمياه ( اور امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ نہیں ساقط کریں گے مگر وہ جو متعین کرے۔ وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر حقوق متعین کرے کہ فلاں فلاں حق ضلع اور مبارات سے ساقط ہوں گے تو وہ حقوق ساقط ہوں گے باقی نہیں۔ اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابراهيم قال للمختلعة السكنى والنفقة (الف) (مصنف ابن ابي هيبته ۱۱۴ ما قالوا فی المختلفة تكون لها نفقه ام لا؟ ج رابع ص ۱۲۷ نمبر ۱۸۳۸۹ر مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب نفقة المختلعة الحامل ج سادس ، ص ۵۰۸ نمبر ۱۱۸۶۵) اس نے معلوم ہوا کہ شرط لگائے تو ساقط ہوں گے ورنہ نہیں۔ حاشیہ : (الف) ابراہیم نے فرمایا ضلع کرنے والی کو سکنی اور نفقہ ملے گا۔ )
(15) And mubara'ah is like khul'. And khul' and mubara'ah both nullify every right that each of the spouses has against the other arising from marriage, according to Abu Hanifa. (16) And Abu Yusuf said: mubara'ah nullifies (rights). (17) And Muhammad said: both do not nullify except what they specify.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage details an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of khul' (divorce at wife's request with compensation) and mubara'ah (mutual separation with compensation) on marital rights. According to Abu Hanifa, both khul' and mubara'ah nullify all rights between spouses that arise from the marriage contract, such as the wife's right to iddah maintenance (nafaqah), housing (sakan), and unpaid dower (mahr). The reasoning is that in khul', the husband takes compensation from the wife, so it would be contradictory for him to then provide maintenance. He supports this with athar (reports) from Qatada and al-Sha'bi, who ruled that a mukhala'ah (khul' woman) has no right to nafaqah. Abu Yusuf distinguishes between khul' and mubara'ah. He holds that mubara'ah, by its linguistic meaning of mutual freeing, nullifies all rights automatically. In contrast, khul' only nullifies those rights that are explicitly specified in the agreement. This is because the term khul' does not inherently imply a release from all obligations. Muhammad al-Shaybani goes further, stating that neither khul' nor mubara'ah nullify any rights except those that are explicitly mentioned when the contract is made. He cites an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, who affirmed that a mukhala'ah woman is entitled to sakan and nafaqah (unless otherwise agreed). The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that under Abu Hanifa's view, the wife loses all post-divorce financial rights immediately upon khul' or mubara'ah. Under Abu Yusuf's view, this only applies to mubara'ah; for khul', the wife retains unspecified rights. Under Muhammad's view, the wife retains all rights except those explicitly waived. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is typically Muhammad's view, as it restricts the nullification to what is agreed upon, providing more protection for the wife.

Key Terms
المبارأة (al-mubara'ah): A separation where both spouses mutually agree to cancel the marriage contract with compensation; linguistically mutual freeing.
يسقطان (yusqitan): They nullify or cause to fall (rights and obligations).
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance including food, clothing, and shelter during iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, what is the effect of khul' on the wife's right to nafaqah during iddah?
A) Nafaqah is automatically nullified because the husband receives compensation.
B) Nafaqah is nullified only in mubara'ah, not in khul'.
C) Nafaqah is not nullified unless explicitly mentioned in the khul' agreement.
D) Nafaqah is nullified unless the wife returns the compensation.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, khul' and mubara'ah do not automatically nullify marital rights; only those rights explicitly specified in the agreement are waived, protecting the wife's default entitlements.

Definition and Ruling of Dhihar
Definition and prohibitory ruling of dhihar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define dhihar and state the prohibitory ruling regarding intimacy before expiation.

Core Idea
Dhihar prohibits intimacy until expiation — it is not divorce, but a temporary prohibition.

Dhihar is a pre-Islamic practice regulated by Islam. The husband's utterance makes his wife unlawful for him until he performs expiation. This lesson defines dhihar and explains the immediate ruling based on the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of dhihar
The husband says to his wife, 'You are like the back of my mother.' This specific comparison is the defining element.
The back (zahr) is used as a metaphor for the mother's entire body, making her a mahram.
2
Determine the immediate ruling
Upon utterance, the wife becomes haram (unlawful) for the husband. He cannot have intercourse, touch, or kiss her.
The prohibition is immediate and does not require intention.
3
Identify the condition for re-permissibility
The husband must perform expiation (kaffarah) before any intimacy. The expiation options are freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people.
The Qur'an explicitly requires expiation 'before they touch each other' (Surah al-Mujadilah 58:4).
4
Confirm the proof from the Qur'an
The verse commands expiation before 'tamass' (touching), proving that intimacy is haram until expiation is performed. The imperative form indicates obligation.
This is a qat'i dalil (definitive evidence) in thubut and dalalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اذا قال الزوج لامرأته انت على كظهر امى فقد حرمت عليه لا يحل له وطؤها ولا مسها ولا تقبيلها حتى يكفر عن ظهاره
If the husband says to his wife, 'You are to me like the back of my mother,' then she has become unlawful to him; it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with her, nor to touch her, nor to kiss her, until he expiates for his dhihar.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling of dhihar (ظهار). Dhihar is a form of pre-Islamic divorce where a husband declares his wife unlawful by comparing her to a mahram relative. In Islam, this declaration does not result in permanent separation but makes her unlawful for the husband until he performs expiation (kaffarah). The prohibitions include intercourse (وطؤها), touching (مسها), and kissing (تقبيلها)—all actions that lead to or constitute intimacy. The expiation is obligatory before any such actions are resumed. The text emphasizes that the prohibition is immediate upon utterance, even if the husband did not intend the legal consequences. The evidence for this ruling is the Qur'anic verse in Surah al-Mujadilah (58:4) and the hadith of Khulaylah bint Malik.

Key Terms
ظهار (dhihar): A husband saying to his wife that she is like the back of his mother or another mahram, making her haram until expiation.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation required for dhihar: freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the immediate legal effect when a husband says to his wife, 'You are like the back of my mother'?
A) The wife becomes divorced with one talaq.
B) The wife becomes haram for the husband until he gives expiation.
C) The marriage is annulled automatically.
D) No effect until the husband intends dhihar.

Key Takeaway
Dhihar prohibits intimacy until expiation — it is not divorce, but a temporary prohibition that requires kaffarah.

Intercourse Before Expiation in Dhihar: No Additional Expiation Required
Ruling on intercourse before expiation in dhihar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a husband who has intercourse before completing dhihar expiation and identify the basis for the ruling.

Core Idea
Premature intercourse in dhihar is a sin but does not require a second expiation.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in dhihar: the husband has intercourse with his wife before performing the required expiation. The text provides the clear ruling that no additional expiation is needed; he must seek forgiveness and fulfill the original expiation. Understanding this ruling helps differentiate between a sin that requires repentance and a legal obligation that is not duplicated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is intercourse before expiation. The ruling is that it is haram but does not necessitate an additional expiation; the original expiation suffices.
Distinguish between the sin (requires repentance) and the legal obligation (expiation remains single).
2
Locate the basis in tradition
The ruling is based on the hadith of Salama ibn Sakhr al-Bayadi, where the Prophet ﷺ required only one expiation even after premature intercourse.
This is a sahih hadith relied upon in the Hanafi school.
3
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some Shafi'i scholars hold that a second expiation may be required, but the Hanafi view is that one suffices.
The difference arises from interpretation of whether the prohibition compounds the obligation.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, the husband must repent (istighfar) and then perform the original expiation (e.g., freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding 60 poor).
No extra expiation is needed even if intercourse repeated before expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان وطئها قبل ان يكفر استغفر الله ولا شيء عليه غير الكفارة الاولى
If he has intercourse with her before he expiates, he should seek forgiveness from Allah and nothing else is upon him besides the first expiation.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling if the husband has intercourse with his wife before performing the expiation for dhihar. The default obligation is to perform the expiation before any intimacy. However, if he transgresses and does so, no additional expiation is required. He must seek forgiveness from Allah (استغفر الله) and perform the original expiation. This is based on the hadith of Salama ibn Sakhr al-Bayadi, in which the Prophet (ﷺ) ruled that only one expiation is necessary even if intercourse occurs prematurely. The ruling indicates that the sin of premature intercourse is not compounded with an extra expiation, though it is a sin requiring repentance.

Key Terms
استغفر الله (istaghfara Allah): Seek forgiveness from Allah for the sin of premature intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must a man do if he has intercourse with his wife after declaring dhihar but before paying expiation?
A) He must perform two expiations.
B) He must divorce his wife.
C) He must seek forgiveness and perform the original expiation.
D) He must free a slave immediately.

Key Takeaway
Intercourse before expiation in dhihar is a sin but does not double the expiation; one expiation and repentance suffice.

Prohibition of Repeated Intercourse Before Expiation in Dhihar
Prohibition of repeated intercourse before expiation in dhihar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding repeated intercourse before expiation in dhihar and identify its supporting evidence.

Core Idea
Repeated intercourse before expiation is haram; the prohibition remains until expiation is completed.

This short ruling addresses a common scenario: after a husband has had intercourse with his wife during the period of dhihar, can he do so again before expiating? The text delivers an unambiguous prohibition. Understanding this rule highlights the seriousness of dhihar and the necessity of completing expiation before normal marital relations resume.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify that the text commands the husband not to repeat intercourse until expiation is performed.
The ruling is haram.
2
Identify the condition
Note that the prohibition is conditional upon not having expiated. Once expiation is complete, the prohibition lifts.
3
Recognize the continuous nature
The prohibition applies to any further acts after the first intercourse; it is not a one-time restriction but continues until expiation.
4
Connect to the hadith evidence
Recall the hadith of Ibn Abbas where the Prophet (ﷺ) instructed a man to stay away from his wife until expiation.
This provides the basis for the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يعاود حتى يكفر
And he should not repeat [intercourse] until he expiates.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This brief ruling prohibits the husband from having intercourse again after the first occurrence until he has performed the expiation. The prohibition of intimacy continues after a single act of intercourse; the sin is not nullified without expiation. The evidence is the hadith of Ibn Abbas, in which the Prophet (ﷺ) told a man who had intercourse after dhihar to not approach his wife until he does what Allah commanded (i.e., expiation). Thus, the husband must refrain from any further intimacy until he completes the expiation (freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor).

Key Terms
يعاود (yu'awidu): To repeat or resume intercourse after having done so once before expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prohibition ends and he can continue without expiation.
B) He must not repeat intercourse until he expiates.
C) He must repeat the dhihar declaration.
D) He must divorce her.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of intercourse before expiation in dhihar remains in effect until expiation is performed; any repetition before expiation is haram.

Definition of 'Awd in Dhihar
Defines the 'awd that necessitates expiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of 'awd in dhihar and explain why expiation is wajib upon the husband's resolve, not upon actual intercourse.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'awd is the resolve to resume relations — expiation is due at the moment of intention, not the act.

This lesson unpacks a concise but crucial definition from the Hanafi school: what exactly is the 'return' ('awd) that triggers expiation (kaffarah) after a dhihar declaration? The answer hinges on the word 'azm (firm resolve). Understanding this distinction clarifies when the obligation actually falls and highlights a significant difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines the term 'awd ('return') as it appears in the context of dhihar. Note that 'awd is a legal concept with a specific technical meaning.
The Arabic root ع و د implies returning to something.
2
Extract the condition that triggers the ruling
The passage states that 'awd is 'that he resolves to have intercourse with her.' The key word is 'azm (firm resolve). This is the condition that makes expiation wajib.
'Azm is a mental act, not a physical one.
3
Understand the legal effect
The legal effect is that expiation (kaffarah) becomes wajib. This obligation is immediate upon the resolve, even before any physical act.
This is the Hanafi position from the zahir al-riwayah.
4
Compare with the opposing school
The Shafi'i school defines 'awd as actual intercourse, not mere intention. This difference leads to different timings for when expiation becomes due.
The thamarah (practical consequence) is significant: under Hanafi, a husband must expiate as soon as he decides to resume relations; under Shafi'i, only after intercourse.
5
Apply the definition in a scenario
Consider a husband who declares dhihar and later, without intercourse, tells his wife he wishes to resume marital life. According to Hanafi law, he has committed 'awd and must expiate before intercourse.
This scenario demonstrates the practical application of the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعود الذى يجب به الكفارة هو ان يعزم على وطيها
And the 'awd (return) that necessitates expiation is that he resolves to have intercourse with her.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage clarifies a key legal concept: the 'awd (عود) that makes expiation wajib (obligatory) is not the actual act of intercourse, but rather the firm resolve (عزم) to have intercourse with the wife after the dhihar declaration. The moment the husband decides to resume marital relations, the expiation becomes due. This is important because the Qur'anic verse (58:4) mentions 'thumma ya'udoon' (then they return), and the Hanafi interpretation is that 'return' here means the intention to return to normal marital relations. This ruling is based on understanding the word 'awd as a determined intention, as opposed to the physical act. Thus, if a husband makes dhihar and then resolves to have intercourse, he must expiate even before actually doing so.

Key Terms
عود ('awd): The return to normal marital relations, defined here as the firm intention to have intercourse.
عزم ('azm): Firm resolve or determination, which triggers the obligation of expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the 'awd that necessitates expiation?
A) The actual act of intercourse.
B) The husband's firm resolve to have intercourse.
C) The wife's consent to resume relations.
D) A period of one month after dhihar.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the 'awd that triggers expiation in dhihar is the husband's firm resolve ('azm) to resume intercourse, not the act itself.

Zihar Beyond the Back: Belly, Thigh, Vagina
Ruling expanding zihar to other prohibited body parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for what utterances constitute zihar when a husband compares his wife to a prohibited body part other than the back.

Core Idea
Zihar is established by any comparison to a female relative's body part that is unlawful to look at.

This passage addresses a common misconception about zihar. While the term 'zihar' derives from 'zahr' (back), Hanafi jurists ruled that the effective cause is the prohibition of looking at the body part. Therefore, comparing one's wife to the mother's belly, thigh, or vagina also constitutes zihar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The husband makes a comparison: 'You are upon me like the belly of my mother' or similar.
The comparison is to a permanently forbidden relative (mother).
2
Recognize the legal consequence
The passage states that he becomes a muzahhir (one who performs zihar).
This triggers the obligation of expiation (kaffarah) before resuming marital relations.
3
Understand the effective cause ('illa)
The 'illa is that the body part is unlawful to look at (haram al-nazar). Since belly, thigh, and vagina are all such parts, the comparison counts as zihar.
The literal word 'back' is not required; the legal cause is broader.
4
Note the expansion from the literal word
The passage expands the definition beyond the specific word 'back' to any prohibited body part.
This is the standard Hanafi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال انت على كبطن امي او كفخذها او كفرجها فهو مظاهر
And if he said, 'You are upon me like the belly of my mother, or like her thigh, or like her vagina,' then he is a muzahhir.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that expands the definition of zihar (ظهار) beyond the specific word 'back' (ظهر). The ruling states that if a husband compares his wife to his mother's belly, thigh, or vagina, he becomes a muzahhir. The Urdu commentary explains that zihar is derived from 'zahr' (back), but the effective cause ('illa) is the prohibition of looking at those body parts. Since looking at these parts is also haram, the comparison qualifies as zihar. The principle is that any comparison to a body part of the mother that is unlawful to view constitutes zihar. This is a standard Hanafi position from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
ظهار (zihar): A pre-Islamic form of divorce where a husband compares his wife to a permanently forbidden female relative (like his mother's back); legally, it is a prohibited but valid pronouncement that requires expiation (kaffarah) to resume marital relations.
مظاهر (muzahhir): The one who performs zihar by making the comparison.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is not zihar because 'thigh' is not 'back'.
B) It is zihar because thigh is a prohibited body part to look at.
C) It is a metaphor but not legally binding.
D) It is talaq.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, zihar is not limited to the word 'back'; any comparison to a female relative's body part that is prohibited to look at is sufficient to establish zihar.

Zihar by Comparing Wife to a Mahram Relative
Extends zihar to comparisons with permanently forbidden female relatives (mahram)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which comparisons to mahram relatives constitute zihar according to the Hanafi school and apply the ruling using the evidence from the athar.

Core Idea
Comparing your wife to any permanently forbidden mahram relative (sister, aunt, foster mother) constitutes zihar, requiring expiation.

In this lesson, we explore an extension of the zihar ruling. The Hanafi school holds that zihar is not limited to comparing one's wife to one's mother. Rather, it applies to any female relative who is permanently forbidden in marriage (mahram), such as a sister or foster mother. The proof comes from an athar narrated by 'Ata'. Understanding this expansion is crucial for correctly applying the rules of zihar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the husband comparing his wife to a mahram relative. The ruling is that this constitutes zihar, making the wife forbidden until expiation.
Zihar is a pre-Islamic practice of saying 'You are like my mother's back' but is extended here.
2
Identify the 'illa (effective cause)
The 'illa is the permanent prohibition of marriage and looking at that relative, similar to the mother. This creates the legal analogy.
The 'illa is derived from the athar.
3
Locate the dalil
The dalil is an athar from 'Ata' reported in Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, which explicitly equates zihar with a mahram relative to zihar with the mother.
Although mursal, it is accepted in Hanafi fiqh as supporting evidence.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) require the word 'mother' explicitly. The Hanafi school expands to all permanent mahrams based on the 'illa.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
5
Apply the ruling
Any such comparison triggers the prohibition. The husband must perform expiation (fasting two months or feeding sixty poor) before resuming marital relations.
If he does not expiate, he remains in sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك ان شبهها بمن لا يحل له النظر اليها على سبيل التابيد من محارمه مثل اخته او عمته او امه من الرضاعة
And similarly if he compares her to someone it is not permissible for him to look at forever among his mahrams, such as his sister, his paternal aunt, or his foster mother.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that extends zihar to comparisons with any female relative who is permanently forbidden in marriage (mahram). The Urdu commentary cites an athar from 'Ata' in Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq: 'Whoever performs zihar with a mahram relative of the womb or a foster sister, all of that is like the mother; she will not be lawful until he expiates.' The basis is that the prohibition of looking and marriage is permanent, similar to the mother. The Hanafi position is that such comparisons fully constitute zihar, requiring expiation if the husband wishes to resume relations. This is consistent with the broader principle that the 'illa (effective cause) is the permanent prohibition.

Key Terms
محارم (mahram): Individuals with whom marriage is permanently forbidden due to blood, fosterage, or marriage ties; in this context, female mahrams like sister, aunt, and foster mother.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife, 'You are like my sister's back,' does this constitute zihar according to the Hanafi school?
A) No, because the sister is not the mother.
B) Yes, because the sister is a permanent mahram.
C) Yes, but only if he says 'back' explicitly.
D) No, because the sister is a different relative.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, zihar extends to any permanently forbidden mahram relative, making expiation necessary.

Zihar When Comparing a Part of the Wife's Body
Ruling on zihar by comparing a part of the wife's body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zihar when a husband compares a part of his wife's body to his mother's back, and explain the qiyas proof using the analogy to talaq.

Core Idea
Comparing a part of the wife to the mother's back triggers full zihar, just as saying 'your finger is divorced' divorces the entire wife.

The husband's statement comparing a part of the wife's body to his mother's back is treated as zihar covering the entire wife. This ruling is derived by analogy to the principle in talaq where mentioning a part results in the whole being divorced. This lesson breaks down the analogy and its implications.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
What is the hukm when a husband says 'your head is like my mother's back'? It constitutes zihar, making the wife prohibited until expiation.
2
Identify the far' (new case)
The husband's statement focuses on a specific part of the wife's body (e.g., head, face, neck).
3
Identify the asl (original case)
In talaq, if a man says 'your finger is divorced,' the entire wife is divorced. This is the established ruling from an athar of Qatada.
Athar from Qatada in Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq
4
Extract the illa (effective cause)
The part mentioned is customarily taken as representative of the whole person. This is the common 'illah.
Illa is jaliyyah (clear) based on custom.
5
Apply qiyas
Since the same illah exists in the far' (mentioning a part in zihar), the ruling is transferred: full zihar applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كذلک ان قال رأسک علی کظهر امي او فرجک او وجهک او رقبتک او نصفک او ثلثک
And similarly if he said, 'Your head is like the back of my mother,' or 'your vagina,' or 'your face,' or 'your neck,' or 'your half,' or 'your third.'
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that covers zihar when the husband compares a part of the wife's body (head, vagina, face, neck, half, third) to the mother's back. The Urdu commentary explains that these parts are commonly used to refer to the whole person. Therefore, saying 'your head is like my mother's back' is treated as comparing the entire wife to the mother, resulting in zihar. Similarly, saying 'half of you' or 'third of you' also constitutes zihar because they refer to a part representing the whole. The proof is an analogy (qiyas) to talaq: if a man says 'your finger is divorced,' the entire wife is divorced (as per the athar from Qatada). By the same reasoning, if a part is used for zihar, the whole zihar applies.

Key Terms
نصف (nisf): Half of the wife's body; when used in zihar, it implies the whole body.
ثلث (thulth): Third of the wife's body; similarly implies the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No, because the face is not a prohibited part.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, comparing any part of the wife that customarily represents the whole to the mother's back establishes full zihar, requiring expiation.

Intention When Saying 'You Are Like My Mother'
Determines legal effect of ambiguous phrase based on husband's intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three possible legal outcomes of the husband's intention when using the ambiguous phrase 'you are like my mother' and apply the rule that intention governs ambiguous utterances in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
An ambiguous phrase like 'you are like my mother' derives its legal effect from the husband's intention alone.

In Hanafi fiqh, not every utterance of 'you are like my mother' triggers zihar or divorce. The context and the speaker's intent are crucial. This lesson examines a clear ruling from the matn: when a husband uses such an ambiguous expression, his stated intention—whether honor, zihar, or divorce—determines the legal outcome. We will explore each scenario and understand why the husband's word is accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous expression
The husband says 'You are upon me like my mother.' The word 'back' (zahr) is implied but not stated, making the phrase ambiguous.
In classical Arabic, zihar traditionally uses the word 'back' (zahr).
2
Recognize the role of intention (niyyah)
Hanafi law refers the adjudication of ambiguous statements to the husband's declared intention. The judge accepts his claim unless proven otherwise.
This is based on the principle that a person's inner state is known only to Allah and themselves.
3
Analyze the three possibilities
If he says he meant honor, no legal effect. If he meant zihar, it becomes zihar (requiring expiation). If he meant talaq, it becomes talaq ba'in (irrevocable divorce).
Each intention yields a different ruling, emphasizing the weight of intention in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Understand the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that the husband's statement of intention is accepted. Other schools may differ.
This is a case of mukhtalaf fih, but the Hanafi position is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال انت على مثل امي يرجع الى نيته فان قال اردت به الكرامة فهو كما قال وان قال اردت الظهار فهو ظهار وان قال اردت الطلاق فهو طلاق بائن
And if he said, 'You are upon me like my mother,' one refers to his intention. If he said, 'I meant honor by it,' then it is as he said. If he said, 'I meant zihar,' then it is zihar. If he said, 'I meant divorce,' then it is a final (ba'in) divorce.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that addresses an ambiguous expression: when a husband says 'you are like my mother' without specifying 'back' or 'belly,' the legal outcome depends on his intention (niyyah). If he claims he meant honor and respect, his word is accepted, and no divorce or zihar occurs. If he intended zihar, then it becomes zihar with its requirements. If he intended talaq, it becomes a talaq ba'in (irrevocable divorce). The Urdu commentary notes that the phrase can be interpreted as missing the word 'back' (like 'you are like the back of my mother'), so intention clarifies. This shows the importance of subjective intent in Hanafi fiqh for ambiguous utterances.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): The internal intention of the husband that determines the legal effect of an ambiguous expression.
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): A final, irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is zihar because the phrase is unambiguous.
B) It is talaq ba'in.
C) It is treated as honor and no divorce or zihar occurs.
D) It is considered a joke with no effect.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an ambiguous utterance like 'you are like my mother' is resolved by the husband's declared intention, and his word is accepted.

Zihar Requires Intention
Ruling statement: intention is a condition for Zihar effectiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that Zihar without intention is void and apply it to determine the validity of a marital pronouncement.

Core Idea
Intention is the decisive factor: without it, Zihar words are legally meaningless.

When a husband utters words that resemble Zihar, the legal outcome depends entirely on his intention. This lesson examines a concise ruling from the Hanafi tradition: if the husband had no intention to perform Zihar, then nothing occurs. This principle safeguards against unintended legal consequences and underscores the centrality of niyyah in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance
The husband's words are 'you are like my mother' or similar. Determine what legal category they belong to: Zihar, divorce, or something else.
The literal words alone do not determine the ruling.
2
Investigate the intention
Did the husband intend Zihar, divorce, or nothing? Intention can be inferred from context or his own admission.
In Hanafi fiqh, intention is a condition for many marital pronouncements.
3
Apply the rule
If no intention for Zihar, then no Zihar occurs. If intention is present, Zihar takes effect with its consequences (prohibition and expiation).
The text explicitly states: 'if he has no intention, then it is nothing.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان لم تكن له نية فليس بشيء [۲۰۳۲] (۱۱) اور اگر کچھ نیت نہ ہو تو کچھ واقع نہیں ہوگی۔ کچھ نیت نہ ہو تو احترام پر عمل کیا جائے گا اور طلاق یا ظہار کچھ واقع نہیں ہوگا۔
And if he has no intention, then it is nothing. [2032] (11) And if there is no intention, then nothing will occur. If there is no intention, then respect will be acted upon, and neither divorce nor Zihar will occur.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This is a ruling statement: Zihar does not take effect without the husband's specific intention to perform Zihar. The author emphasizes that if the husband utters words resembling Zihar but without intending Zihar (e.g., he intended divorce or nothing), then no Zihar occurs. The default is to respect the literal meaning of words unless there is a clear intention to the contrary. This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that in ambiguous utterances, intention determines the legal outcome.

Key Terms
الظهار (al-ẓihār): A pre-Islamic practice where a husband says to his wife 'you are like my mother's back', which in Islam is considered a prohibited form of divorce that triggers expiation.
نية (niyyah): Intention; the mental resolve that determines the legal nature of an utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zihar occurs.
B) Nothing occurs.
C) Divorce occurs.
D) It depends on the tone.

Key Takeaway
Intention (niyyah) is a necessary condition for Zihar to be effective; without it, the utterance is void.

Zihar Only with Wife, Not Slave-Girl
Specifies that Zihar applies only to a wife, not a slave-girl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that Zihar is only valid with a wife and identify the Quranic evidence that restricts Zihar to wives.

Core Idea
Zihar is only valid with a wife; a slave-girl is not a wife, so Zihar does not apply.

This lesson clarifies that the pre-Islamic practice of Zihar (declaring one's wife like one's mother's back) has a specific legal scope in Islam. The Quranic verse and the consensus of the Companions restrict Zihar to valid wives, excluding slave-girls. Understanding this boundary prevents incorrect application of Zihar rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing Zihar on a slave-girl. The ruling is that it does not constitute Zihar; no legal consequences follow.
The text starts by stating the ruling directly.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is Quran 58:2: '...those who pronounce Zihar among their wives...' The word 'wives' is key.
The verse is quoted in the commentary.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Arabic phrase 'min nisaa'ihim' (from their wives) specifies that Zihar is only from wives. A slave-girl is not a wife.
Linguistic analysis of the term.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is a minority view (some Hanbalis) that Zihar may apply to slave-girls, but the majority including Hanafi, Shafi'i, Maliki restrict to wives.
Mukhtalaf fih but not a dispute within Hanafi.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the apparent meaning of the verse and the athar of Ibn Abbas confirming there is no Zihar for a slave-girl.
No explicit tarjih authority mentioned, but the ruling is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يكون الظهار الا من زوجته فان ظاهر من امته لم يكن مظاهرا [۲۰۳۳] (۱۲) اور نہیں ہوگا ظہار مگر اپنی بیوی سے، پس اگر ظہار کیا اپنی باندی سے تو ظہار کرنے والا نہیں ہوگا۔ آیت میں ہے کہ بیوی سے ظہار کرے اس لئے باندی سے ظہار نہیں ہوگا۔ والذين بظاهرون من نسائهم ثم يعودون (آيت سورة المجادلۃ ۵۸) اس میں ہے کہ بیوی سے ظہار کرے اور باندی بیوی نہیں ہوتی اس لئے اس سے ظہار نہیں ہوگا (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس قـال مـن شـاء باهلته انه ليس للامة ظهار (الف) (سن للبیہقی ، باب لاظہار فی الامته ج سابع ص ۶۳۰ نمبر ۰ ۱۵۲۵)
And Zihar is not except from his wife. If he performs Zihar from his slave-girl, he is not a Muzahir. [2033] (12) And Zihar will not occur except from his wife. So if he performs Zihar from his slave-girl, he will not be a Muzahir. The verse says that Zihar is from the wife, therefore from a slave-girl it will not occur. And those who pronounce Zihar among their wives and then return [to what they said] (Surah Al-Mujadilah 58). In this it is that Zihar is from the wife, and a slave-girl is not a wife, so Zihar is not from her. (2) There is an Athar from Ibn Abbas: 'Whoever wishes, I can enter into Mubahalah with him that there is no Zihar for a slave-girl.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab La Zihar fi al-Ammah, vol. 7, p. 630, no. 1525)
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof: Zihar is specifically for wives, not for slave-girls (umm walad or ordinary bondwomen). The Quranic verse 'who pronounce Zihar among their wives' (min nisa'ihim) restricts Zihar to wives. A slave-girl is not a wife, so Zihar does not apply. The hadith of Ibn Abbas confirms this by stating that there is no Zihar for a slave-girl, and he challenges anyone to refute him, indicating consensus among the Companions. The Hanafi ruling is based on these texts.

Key Terms
زوجة (zawjah): A wife in a valid marriage contract.
أمة (ammah): A female slave, owned by the master, with whom he may have relations without marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Zihar is only valid when pronounced upon a wife; a slave-girl does not qualify, so no Zihar occurs.

Zihar Against Multiple Wives in One Statement
Ruling on Zihar against multiple wives with one sentence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Zihar against multiple wives and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Each wife is a separate Zihar: one statement to many wives creates multiple kaffarah.

When a husband says 'You are all like my mother' to his wives, does one statement create one Zihar or multiple? The Hanafi school holds that Zihar applies individually to each wife, requiring a separate kaffarah for each. This lesson explores the proof from the Athar of Zuhri, Hasan, and Tawus, and contrasts it with the Shafi'i old opinion based on Athar from Ibn Abbas and Umar.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the legal question: when a husband addresses multiple wives with one Zihar statement, how many Zihar occur and how many kaffarah are required?
2
State the Hanafi position and its dalil
The Hanafi school says each wife is addressed individually, so multiple Zihar and multiple kaffarah. The proof is the Athar from Zuhri, Hasan, and Tawus, and the principle that the plural address applies to each individual.
The Athar explicitly says 'four kaffarah' for four wives.
3
State the opposing position and its dalil
Imam al-Shafi'i's old opinion says one kaffarah suffices, based on the Athar from Ibn Abbas and Umar.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the opposing Athar is weaker, and the stronger evidence (multiple Athar and the general rule) supports multiple kaffarah.
The response is based on the principle that each wife is a separate subject of the Zihar.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi husband pays multiple kaffarah, while a Shafi'i (old opinion) would pay only one.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the position of multiple kaffarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے اپنی بیویوں سے کہا تم لوگ میرے اوپر میری ماں کی طرح ہو تو یہ ظہار کرنے والا ہوگا سب سے، اور شوہر پر ہر ایک کے لئے کفارہ ہے۔ ر شوہر کے پاس مثلا چار بیویاں تھیں، ایک ہی جملے میں چاروں سے کہا تم لوگ میرے اوپر میری ماں کی پیٹھ کی طرح ہو تو سب سے الگ الگ ظہار ہو جائیں گے۔ اور ہر ایک کے لئے الگ الگ کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ اور شوہر کو چار کفارے ادا کرنے ہوں گے۔ (۱) اگر د لرچہ جملہ ایک ہے لیکن بیویاں چار ہیں اس لئے ظہار چار ہوئے۔ اور ہر ظہار کے لئے الگ کفارہ چاہئے اس لئے کفارہ بھی چار لازم ہوں گے (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن الزهري قال اذا ظاهر من اربع نسوة فاربع كفارات وکذلک قال الحسن وطاؤس (الف) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب المظاهر من نساءه فی قول واحدج سادس ص ۴۳۹ نمبر ۱۵۷۰/۱۱۵۶۹ارسن للبیہقی ، باب الرجل يظاھرمن اربع نسوة له بكلمة واحدة ج سابع میں ۶۳۱ نمبر ۱۵۲۵۴) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ چار ظہار ہوں گے اور چار کفارہ دینے ہوں گے۔ امام شافعی کا قول قدیم یہ ہے کہ ایک ہی کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس وعن عمر في رجل ظاهر من اربع نسوة بكلمة قال كفارة واحدة (ج) (سمن بھی باب الرجل يظاهر من اربع نسوة له بكلمة واحدة ج سابع ، ص ۶۳۰ نمبر ۱۵۲۵۳/ مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب المظاهر من نساء و فی قول واحدج
Someone said to his wives: 'You all are like my mother to me', then he is a Muzahir to all of them, and upon the husband is a kaffarah for each one. For example, a man had four wives, and in one sentence he said to all four: 'You all are like the back of my mother to me', then separate Zihar will occur for each, and separate kaffarah will be obligatory for each, and the husband will have to give four kaffarah. (1) Even though the sentence is one, but the wives are four, so Zihar became four, and for each Zihar a separate kaffarah is required, so four kaffarah become obligatory. (2) There is an Athar from Zuhri: 'If one performs Zihar from four wives, then four kaffarah', and similarly said Hasan and Tawus. (Mussanaf Abdur Razzaq, Bab al-Muzahir min Nisa'i fi Qawl Wahid, vol. 6, p. 439, no. 11569; Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab al-Rajul Yuzahir min Arba' Niswa lahu bi Kalimah Wahidah, vol. 7, p. 631, no. 15254) From this Athar it is known that there will be four Zihar and four kaffarah must be given. Imam Shafi'i's old opinion is that one kaffarah is obligatory. There is an Athar from Ibn Abbas and from Umar regarding a man who performed Zihar from four wives with one word: he said one kaffarah. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, same chapter, p. 630, no. 15253; Mussanaf Abdur Razzaq, same).
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position: if a husband says to his multiple wives in one statement 'you are all like my mother', separate Zihar occurs for each wife, and each Zihar requires its own separate kaffarah. This is based on the principle that Zihar is an individual act per wife; the word 'kull' or plural address applies to each individually. The Athar from Zuhri, Hasan, and Tawus supports this. Imam Shafi'i (in his old opinion) held that only one kaffarah is required, based on the Athar from Ibn Abbas and Umar. The Hanafi response: the Athar from Umar and Ibn Abbas is not preferable because it is contrary to stronger evidence (the general principle of individuality). The practical consequence: a Hanafi husband must pay multiple kaffarah if he uses one statement.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation required for Zihar; it is a sequence of freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.
ظهار (zihār): A pre-Islamic form of divorce forbidden by Islam; it is a husband's saying to his wife that she is like his mother's back, requiring expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man says to his four wives in one sentence 'you are all like my mother', how many kaffarah must he give?
A) One kaffarah
B) Four kaffarah
C) Two kaffarah
D) None, because the statement is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a single Zihar statement addressed to multiple wives creates a separate Zihar for each wife, each requiring its own kaffarah.

Kaffarah of Zihar: Sequential Obligation before Intercourse
Details the three-tier kaffarah for Zihar and its prerequisite before intercourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three-tier kaffarah for Zihar in order and explain the condition that it must be completed before intercourse.

Core Idea
Kaffarah for Zihar is sequential: free a slave, then fast, then feed—all before intercourse.

This passage addresses the expiation required for Zihar, a pre-Islamic practice that Islam reformed. The Quran (58:3-4) prescribes a three-step sequential kaffarah that must be fulfilled before the husband resumes marital relations. The Hanafi school adheres strictly to this order, emphasizing that each step is contingent on inability to perform the previous one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is Zihar, and the ruling is that the husband must perform kaffarah (wajib) before resuming intercourse.
Kaffarah is wajib, not just recommended.
2
Locate the dalil
The Quranic verses (58:3-4) are cited as the primary proof for the ruling.
The verses are qat'i al-thubut and qat'i al-dalalah in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The conjunction 'fa' (then) in the verse indicates a strict sequential order, and the phrase 'min qabli an yatamassa' qualifies all three options with the condition of being before intercourse.
The sequence is derived from the word 'fa' linking each option.
4
Note the ijma' (consensus)
The passage implicitly indicates that the sequence is agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) among the schools, though some may differ on choice if unable? Actually the Hanafi position is strict sequence.
No major ikhtilaf on the sequence itself.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is the sequential order: free a slave if able, else fast two consecutive months, else feed sixty poor.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكفارة الظهار عتق رقبة فان لم يجدد فصيام شهرين متتابعين فمن لم يستطع فاطعام ستين مسكينا کل ذلك قبل المسیس [۲۰۳۵] (۱۴) اور کفارہ ظہار غلام کو آزاد کرنا ہے، پس اگر نہ پائے تو دو ماہ پے در پے روزے رکھنا ہے، پس جو طاقت نہ رکھتا ہو تو ساٹھ مسکینوں کو کھانا کھلانا ہے۔ یہ سب ولی سے پہلے ہو۔ ا کفارہ ادا کرنے کی ترتیب یہ ہے کہ پہلے غلام آزاد کرنے کی کوشش کرے، اس پر قدرت نہ ہو تو پے در پے دو ماہ روزے رکھے، اور اس پر بھی قدرت نہ ہو تو ساٹھ مسکینوں کو کھانا کھلائے ۔ اور یہ سب وطی کرنے سے پہلے کرے پھر وطی کرے۔ آیت اور حدیث میں اسی طرح کفارہ لازم کیا ہے۔ والذين يظاهرون من نسائهم ثم يعودون لماقالوا فتحرير رقبة من قبل يتماسا ذلكم تو عظون به والله بما تعملون خبير فمن لم يجد فصيام شهرين متتابعين من قبل ان يتماسا فمن لم يستطع فاطعام ستين مسكينا (الف) ( آیت ۴٫۳ سورۃ المجادلہ (۵۸) اس آیت میں کفارہ کی تفصیل اوپر کی ترتیب کے ساتھ ہے۔ اور یہ بھی ذکر ہے کہ وہلی سے پہلے کفارہ دے۔
And the kaffarah of Zihar is freeing a slave; if he does not find, then fasting two consecutive months; and if he is unable, then feeding sixty poor persons. All that before touching. [2035] (14) And the kaffarah of Zihar is to free a slave; if he does not find, then to fast two consecutive months; then if he does not have the ability, then to feed sixty poor persons. All this before intercourse. The order is: first try to free a slave; if unable, then fast two consecutive months; if also unable, then feed sixty poor. And all this before intercourse, then have intercourse. The verse and hadith make kaffarah obligatory in this way: 'Those who pronounce Zihar among their wives and then return to what they said, then freeing a slave before they touch; that is what you are admonished with, and Allah is All-Aware of what you do. And whoever does not find, then fasting two consecutive months before they touch; and whoever is unable, then feeding sixty poor people.' (Surah Al-Mujadilah 58:3-4) In this verse the detail of kaffarah is with the above order, and it also mentions that kaffarah (freeing or fasting) is before intercourse.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the Quran. The kaffarah for Zihar is a sequential obligation: (1) freeing a slave, (2) if unable, fasting two consecutive months, (3) if unable, feeding sixty poor persons. All must be performed before the husband resumes sexual relations with his wife (mass). The Quranic verse (58:3-4) explicitly states this order and the condition 'before they touch'. The Hanafi school follows this as an obligatory sequence. If one is capable of freeing a slave, he cannot skip to fasting; each step is contingent on inability to perform the previous one.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation required for Zihar; it is a sequence of freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor, all before intercourse.
المسيس (al-masīs): Sexual intercourse; the time before which kaffarah must be completed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Must be accepted by the wife

Key Takeaway
The kaffarah for Zihar is a strict sequential obligation — free a slave, then fast, then feed — all before intercourse.
